Received 
      29th January 2020
    First published on 6th April 2020AbstractOwing to their large ratio of surface area to mass and volume, metal–organic frameworks and porous carbons have revolutionized many applications that rely on chemical and physical interactions at surfaces. However, a major challenge today is to shape these porous materials to translate their enhanced performance from the laboratory into macroscopic real-world applications. In this review, we give a comprehensive overview of how the precise morphology control of metal oxides can be transferred to metal–organic frameworks and porous carbon materials. As such, tailored material structures can be designed in 0D, 1D, 2D, and 3D with considerable implications for applications such as in energy storage, catalysis and nanomedicine. Therefore, we predict that major research advances in morphology control of metal–organic frameworks and porous carbons will facilitate the use of these materials in addressing major needs of the society, especially the grand challenges of energy, health, and environment.
Jongkook Hwang
Jongkook Hwang received his PhD from Pohang University of Science and Technology (POSTECH) under the supervision of Prof. Jinwoo Lee in 2016. After his first post-doctoral training at the Max Planck Institute of Colloids and Interfaces, he moved to Utrecht University where he worked on development of heterogeneous catalysts for preferential CO oxidation in H2. Recently, he was appointed as an assistant professor in department of chemical engineering at Ajou University, Korea. His research interest focuses on the synthesis of nanoporous materials with well-defined structures and morphologies for energy- and catalytic applications.
Aleksander Ejsmont
Aleksander Ejsmont received his BS (2016) and MSc (2018) from collaborated universities: Adam Mickiewicz University in Poznań (AMU), Military University of Technology in Warsaw and Lodz University of Technology, followed by a one-year postgraduate research at the University of Central Lancashire. In 2019 he started his PhD studies at AMU under the supervision of Prof. Joanna Goscianska. His research focuses on carbon materials, their synthesis, modification and applicability in fields such as adsorption, drug delivery and environmental sciences.
Ralph Freund
Ralph Freund is currently a PhD student at the University of Augsburg (Germany) under the supervision of Prof. Dirk Volkmer. He obtained his MSc degree in chemistry from the University of Munich (LMU, Germany) with Stefan Wuttke in 2018, investigating the chemistry of metal oxide to metal–organic framework conversion reactions. His current research focuses on electric-field responsive porous-coordination frameworks constructed from pyrazolate linkers.
Joanna Goscianska
Joanna Goscianska is a professor at the Faculty of Chemistry of Adam Mickiewicz University in Poznań (AMU). She obtained her Master's degree in 2005 and doctoral degree in 2009 in the field of chemistry. During her PhD studies, she participated in three research internships at Laboratoire Catalyse & Spectrochimie in Caen (France). In 2018 she became a laureate of the habilitation fellowship “L’ORÉAL-UNESCO FOR WOMEN IN SCIENCE”. Her principal research is focused on the synthesis, modification, and characterization of porous materials (e.g. metal oxides, ordered mesoporous silica and carbons) and their application in adsorption processes, catalysis, and drug delivery systems.
Bernhard V. K. J. Schmidt
Bernhard V. K. J. Schmidt completed his PhD in 2013 with Prof. Barner-Kowollik at the Karlsruhe Institute of Technology and a Post Doc with Prof. Hawker at the University of California, Santa Barbara. Afterwards he joined the department of Prof. Antonietti at the Max Planck Institute of Colloids and Interfaces as a Group Leader. Recently, he was appointed as a Lecturer in Synthetic Polymer Chemistry at the University of Glasgow. His research focuses on block copolymer self-assembly, metal–organic framework/polymer hybrids and carbon nitride/polymer hybrid materials.
Stefan Wuttke
Stefan Wuttke created the research group “Wuttkegroup for Science”, initially hosted at the Institute of Physical Chemistry at the University of Munich (LMU, Germany). Currently, he is an Ikerbasque Professor at the Basque Center for Materials, Applications and Nanostructures (BCMaterials, Spain). His research is focused on developing methodologies to write and read chemical information onto and from the backbone of hybrid framework materials. In addition, his research interests also include the acquisition of a fundamental understanding of the chemical and physical processes involved in their synthesis and functionalization.
1. Introduction
Porous materials are of high interest for diverse applications ranging from catalysis and separation to gas storage and sensing. These materials can interact with their surrounding not only on their external surface, but throughout the vast internal surface of the pores. By functionalizing the internal and/or external surface, the activity can be tailored to the application. However, a great challenge today is to build materials that translate the functionality at the molecular level effectively to the macroscopic world. To do this, effective transport of reagents to, from, and within porous materials is critical. To overcome this barrier, porous materials will need to be structured with feature sizes from below one micrometre to the millimetre or centimetre scale. In fact, the challenge of morphology control over multiple length scales (Fig. 1) of porous materials has been overlooked by researchers so far but it is the key to enable groundbreaking real-world applications.
 Fig. 1  Illustrating the challenge to synthesize a macroscopic object (e.g. toy frog, right) with the same morphology at the mesoscale (light microscopy image, middle) and at the nanoscale (scanning electron microscopy image, left). 
Recent years have seen a surge in work on materials that are based on reticular chemistry.1–8 In reticular chemistry, molecular building blocks with controlled shape, geometry, functionality and valence are connected with strong chemical bonds to form porous, crystalline framework structures. Organic building blocks can then be assembled with secondary building units (SBU, e.g. metal-oxo clusters) to form extended materials called metal–organic frameworks (MOFs). The nodes and vertices of reticular materials are designed in such a way that they are rigid and directional, which is of great importance as this enables structure prediction and design. The rigidity and directionality of the components also make these materials porous, as free voids in the materials are easily created. These free voids can be post-synthetically modified to add different functionalities to the material.6,8 The accessibility of the interior of these materials to guests, in addition to the design and functionality aspect, has led to tremendous interest in reticular materials as they are inherently useful for gas adsorption, catalysis, sensing, and drug delivery.6–9 However, the morphology control of MOF materials beyond their periodic and designed networks is the current key challenge that has to be addressed in order to translate the scientific innovation of reticular chemistry from the lab bench into applicable technologies.10
The synthesis of a MOF material always starts with the strategic selection of precursor materials. Besides an organic building block, an inorganic compound providing metal ions is necessary, which most commonly is a metal salt, as it easily dissolves. A relatively new and different approach is the use of metal oxides (MOs). Even though their use is challenging due to low solubility issues and slow and non-homogeneous conversion, by carefully optimizing the experimental conditions the MO-to-MOF conversion can be carried out preserving the morphology of the MO. Thus, the use of MO precursors, which offers a huge number of different morphologies that can be synthesized with high precision, potentially provides an easy and smart way for the precise synthesis of different MOF morphologies. This strategy opens the door to morphologies that cannot be achieved using reactions based on metal salt precursors or other approaches.
Moreover, the careful carbonization of those MOF materials offers to translate this morphology control to porous carbon.11 MOF materials could be used as “all-in-one” templates/precursors that contain both inorganic and organic constituents which can be pre-selected for preparation of targeted porous carbon materials, so called MOF-derived carbon (MDC), with specific morphologies and a plethora of applications, e.g. in energy storage, catalysis, adsorption/separation or pharmacy. MO, MOF and MDC are closely connected, which facilitates synthetic avenues from MO over MOF to MDC with control over morphology from the first building blocks to the final carbon material (Fig. 2). As such, control over MO morphology allows architecture direction over the whole synthesis route. This approach enables additional control over morphology and access to complex material architectures that are challenging to achieve via MOFs alone.12,13
 Fig. 2  Multifaceted morphology of different metal oxides (left) transferred to metal–organic frameworks of different compositions (middle left) and further to metal–organic framework-derived carbon (middle right) allowing systematically controlled synthesis of complex materials for various industrial applications (right). 
In this review, we summarize the morphology control methods of MOF/MDC with particular emphasis on the use of ‘MO as primary architecture directing agents’. Starting from morphology control of MO itself, we cover the transformation of MO into MOF and finally into MDC. To do so, we categorize the reported MO/MOF/MDC products according to their synthetic strategies and particle morphologies (0D, 1D, 2D, 3D and hierarchical architectures). The synthetic methods, mechanisms, and their advantages and limitations are discussed comprehensively. Morphology controlled MOF and MDC materials have already shown great potential in a number of applications such as batteries, supercapacitors, fuel cells, adsorption, catalysis, sensing, and nanomedicine, which will be addressed as well. Overall, the review describes the avenue from MO over MOF to obtain well-defined MDC for relevant applications.
2. Morphology
According to the International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry (IUPAC), morphology is defined as shape, optical appearance, texture or topography of surface and form of phase domains in materials. The expressions morphology and shape are usually used as synonyms when describing the external structure of nanoparticles and their aggregates.14,15 However, the differences between these concepts should be emphasized. The term morphology includes physical size, flatness, roundness, sphericity and aspect ratio of the nanoparticles while the shape considers their appearance without taking into account specific dimensions (Fig. 3).
 Fig. 3  Top: Morphology in the form of 3D particles (left: smooth surface, middle: roughly nanostructure surface, right: smooth surface), bottom: shape in the form of 2D projection (left, middle: spherical, right: square). 
The concept of morphology may seem intuitive until one attempts to determine the appropriate classification system of particles with regular and irregular shapes employing advanced 2D and 3D microscopy imaging techniques.16,17 This system of classification should be as versatile as possible and apply to a wide range of materials. In the case of nanostructures, the greatest difficulty in describing their morphology is that they undergo continuous transformations from one form to another. However, in 1995, Gleiter18 presented the first classification system of nanostructured materials that was explained in detail by Skorokhod et al.19 It was incomplete because it did not include zero-dimensional (0D), one-dimensional (1D), two-dimensional (2D) and three-dimensional (3D) nanostructures, e.g. fullerenes, tubes, and flowers. Thus, Pokropivny and Skorokhod20 developed a modified classification system, in which they took into account the above-mentioned nanostructures. Nanomaterials were classified as 0D, 1D, 2D, and 3D depending on their specific geometrical dimensions. Fig. 4 shows a schematic representation of various nanostructures and plots of energy dependence versus density of states.21 It was found that the degree of confinement determines the electronic density of state for different types of nanostructures.
 Fig. 4  Electronic density of states for 3D, 2D, and 1D nanostructures, and 0D quantum dots. The arrows represent the spatial confinement occurring in each system (Reprinted with permission.21 Copyright 2018 Elsevier). 
2.1. Zero-dimensional nanostructures
Zero-dimensional (0D) nanostructures, e.g., quantum dots, spheres, ellipsoids, and polyhedra, have all dimensions measured within the nanoscale, varying from a few to several dozen nanometres (Fig. 5). They can be amorphous (randomly distributed atoms and molecules) or crystalline (atoms and molecules organized in a defined lattice pattern) and consist of single or more chemical elements.17,21–28
 Fig. 5  TEM (a–c and g–i) and SEM (d–f) images of 0D nanostructures: (a) quantum dots (CdSe/ZnS) (Reprinted with permission.23 Copyright 2013, Inderscience), (b) spherical nanoparticles (Mn3O4), (c) elongated nanocrystals (Mn3O4) (Reprinted with permission.24 Copyright 2010, American Chemical Society), (d and g) Cu octahedron viewed from the [111] axis, (e and h) Cu octahedron viewed from the [112] axis and (f and i) Cu octahedron viewed from the [110] axis (Reprinted with permission.26 Copyright 2015, American Chemical Society). 
Quantum dots with diameters in the range of 2–10 nanometres are shapeless, irregular polyhedra indicating unique electronic properties, which is partly a result of their high surface-to-volume ratio. In this case, facets are not well-developed. The presence of under-coordinated atoms and high surface energy of quantum dots make them more liable to interactions with the surroundings.21–23
Zero-dimensional spheres are formed by reducing the surface energy of various nanomaterials. They exhibit high surface area-to-volume ratio, mechanical strength and good dispersion due to the stabilization of electrostatic charges. Spheres can be created to have well-developed porosity. It is worth noting that spherical morphology is the most stable that occurs in nature. Spheres elongated in one dimension are called ellipsoids. These nanostructures can be unstable because under extreme conditions sharp ends tend to shrink whereas surfaces with smaller curvature grow leading to minimizing surface energy so that they approach spherical geometry.17,21,22,24 Nanomaterials with specific cubic crystal symmetry, enclosed by (100) facets, typically occur in the form of cubes. The high surface energy of cubes related to the presence of under-coordinated atoms is usually lowered by rounding their edges and vertices. Cubes are often employed as building blocks to generate one-dimensional superstructures having well-defined interparticle orientations and regulated electromagnetic properties.21,22 Octahedra together with cubes are the most thoroughly studied nanostructures having interesting physicochemical features. They are composed of eight (111) facets. In 2D microscopic projection, octahedra can be visible as rectangles, rhombuses, and hexagons depending on their orientation. In addition, octahedra are considered classic models for testing the surface properties of (111) facets. However, their precise synthesis is quite difficult.22,26,29
2.2. One-dimensional nanostructures
One-dimensional (1D) nanostructures including rods, wires, tubes, belts, hooks, ribbons, needles, fibres, helices (or spirals) and zigzags are characterized by the high ratio of one dimension over two other, which are equivalent, and in the nanoscale (length ≫ width or diameter).17,21,22,24,25 Many of them are presented in the literature as acicular-like structures whose length can even reach the millimetre range (Fig. 6).22
 Fig. 6  SEM images of 1D structures: (a) rods (γ-MnO2) (Reprinted with permission.30 Copyright 2012, Elsevier), (b) wires (δ-KxV2O5) (Reprinted with permission.31 Copyright 2010, American Chemical Society), (c) large arrays of oriented nanotubes (TiO2) (Reprinted with permission.32 Copyright 2003, American Chemical Society), (d) belts (ZnO) (Reprinted with permission.33 Copyright 2009, Inderscience Enterprises Ltd), (e) helical ribbons and double-layered nanotubes (TiO2) (Reprinted with permission.34 Copyright 2002, American Chemical Society), (f) needles (β-Co(OH)2) (Reprinted with permission.35 Copyright 2008, John Wiley & Sons), (g) hollow fibres (TiO2) (Reprinted with permission.36 Copyright 2000, American Chemical Society), (h) zigzag belts (SnO2) (Reprinted with permission.37 Copyright 2016, Elsevier), and (i) zigzag GaN (Reprinted with permission.38 Copyright 2016, Elsevier). 
Rods exhibiting aspect ratios of 3–5 may occur as aggregates composed of separate building blocks like dots, polyhedra or platelets.22,30 Wires have aspect ratios even more than 1000 and they are described by greater flexibility and slenderness compared to rods.31 The structure of wires is so simple that there is no space for any defects, thus electrons pass through unimpeded. In contrast to rods and wires, tubes have more complex hollow structures and can be single- or multi-walled. Their properties differ depending on how they are rolled up. Belts are extremely thin and flat structures of ribbon shape with a rectangular cross-section. Moreover, they possess high specific surface areas and are therefore often used in catalysis and photocatalysis.25,39,40
Progress in developing new synthesis methods of 1D nanostructures with precisely controlled dimensions, phase purity, morphology, and chemical composition has contributed to their wide application as functional units in the design of solar cells, electronic nanodevices, supercapacitors, gas sensors or transistors. Xia et al.39 have compiled bottom-up synthetic strategies for fabrication of 1D nanostructures which involve: (i) utilization of the intrinsically anisotropic crystallographic structure of a solid to achieve 1D growth, (ii) reduction of the seed symmetry by introducing a liquid–solid interface, (iii) modification of seed growth by control of supersaturation, (iv) a capping reagent assisted procedure in order to kinetically control the growth rates of different facets of seeds, (v) application of appropriate templates exhibiting 1D morphologies to manage the 1D nanostructure creation, (vi) 0D nanostructure self-assembly and (vii) reducing the size of 1D microstructures. It should be noted that a wide variety of materials can naturally grow into 1D nanostructures which is influenced by highly anisotropic bonding in crystallographic structures. They exhibit distinct optical, thermal, chemical and electronic properties. In addition, 1D nanostructures are interesting systems for studying new phenomena at the nanoscale and dependence of specific physicochemical properties on the shape and dimensions.
2.3. Two-dimensional nanostructures
Two-dimensional (2D) nanomaterials, possessing thickness in the range from a few to tens of nanometres and lateral dimension reaching up to many centimetres, have been a subject of intensive research in the last few years.17,21,22,25,41 They are described in the literature as single- or multilayered nanostructures with strong bonds in plane and weak van der Waals forces between layers which are often deposited on the surface of various substrates (e.g. silicon, nickel sheets, copper foil). 2D nanostructures typically include platelets, sheets, flakes, rings, membranes, walls, prisms, discs, and films characterized by unique geometry, well-developed surface area and high surface-to-volume ratio (Fig. 7).25,41,42 In addition, the advantages of 2D nanostructures are also the presence of many active sites, straightforward surface functionalization and the possibility to assemble into 3D complex structures. Up to now, 2D nanostructures of graphene, MOs, polymers, metal hydroxides, metal dichalcogenides, boron nitride, phosphorene, MXenes and MOFs have been synthesized with the use of strategies based on extraction, exfoliation, chemical or physical vapor deposition and various wet chemical methods.41
 Fig. 7  SEM images of 2D structures: (a) plates (porous ZnO) (Reprinted with permission.43 Copyright 2008, John Wiley & Sons), (b) membranes (Fe2O3) (Licensed under CC-BY44), (c) membranes (Pd/MnOx/Pd) (Licensed under CC-BY45), (d) mesocrystal discs (Mn3O4) (Reprinted with permission.46 Copyright 2014, John Wiley & Sons), (e) wall arrays (Ni(OH)2) (Reprinted with permission.47 Copyright 2015, The Royal Chemical Society), (f) flakes (ZnO) (Reprinted with permission.48 Copyright 2013, Elsevier), (g) dense films (NiO) (Reprinted with permission.49 Copyright 2010, Elsevier), (h) diskettes (SnO) (Reprinted with permission.50 Copyright 2003, John Wiley & Sons), and (i) rings (ZnO) (Reprinted with permission.51 Copyright 2006, American Chemical Society). 
In 2D microscopic images, platelets can appear in the form of flat triangles, rectangles, rhombuses or hexagons which are largely dependent on the crystal lattice and growth direction of materials.43 Those having circular or irregular shapes are called discs and discoids.22,46 Films and sheets are 2D nanostructures with very high but not well-defined aspect ratios exhibiting superior mechanical properties which include hardness and strength.41,42,49 It is worth noting that during the formation of films, their interaction with appropriately selected substrate determines the initial nucleation and growth. Three basic modes of initial nucleation involve: (i) island or Volmer–Weber growth, (ii) layer or Frank–van der Merwe growth, and (iii) island-layer or Stranski–Krastonov growth. Island growth takes place when the growth species are more strongly bonded to each other than to the substrate as opposed to layer growth mode. In the latter one, first the monolayer is completely formed before the deposition of the second layer. Island-layer growth is a combination of layer growth and island growth modes.42 Rings, which are one of the least-studied 2D nanostructures, can be fabricated by the rupture of platelets in order to generate a hole in the centre.51
Easy and precise control of 2D nanostructure composition enables exploration and optimization of their functional properties which contribute to the extension of applications in the design of novel sensors, electronic devices, photocatalysts, and reactors.
2.4. Three-dimensional nanostructures
Three-dimensional (3D) nanostructures are described as complex structures assembled from 0D, 1D, 2D or multiscale nanocomponents which have three random dimensions larger than 100 nm. The examples are multipods, balls (dendritic structures), cones, pillars, and hierarchical structures such as flowers, trees, branches, and urchins (Fig. 8).17,21,22,25,35,52,53 Moreover, both spheres and polyhedra (e.g. tetrahedra, cubes, octahedra, prisms) can also be classified into 3D nanostructures if they are polycrystalline and obtained via the aggregation of fundamental building blocks including dots, rods or platelets. Compared with 0D spheres and polyhedra, they are characterized by larger sizes and porosity. Their surfaces can be smooth, rough or occupied by various types of adsorbates. 3D spheres and polyhedra often occur in the form of hollow or multilayered structures.22
 Fig. 8  SEM images of 3D structures: (a) colloidal spheres (Mn3O4) (Reprinted with permission.24 Copyright 2010, American Chemical Society), (b) octahedra (Cu2O), (c) eightpod cubes (Cu2O), (d) six-armed starlike Cu2O (Reprinted with permission.54 Copyright 2009, American Chemical Society), (e) ZnO microsphere consisting of nanorods (Reprinted with permission.55 Copyright 2009, American Chemical Society), (f) doughnut-shaped microparticles (ZnO) (Reprinted with permission.56 Copyright 2005, American Chemical Society), (g) flowers (β-Ni(OH)2) (Reprinted with permission.57 Copyright 2014, The Royal Chemical Society), (h) multipods (Ni) (Reprinted with permission.53 Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society), (i) dendritic structures (γ-Fe2O3) (Reprinted with permission.58 Copyright 2011, American Chemical Society), (j) urchins (VOx) (Reprinted with permission.59 Copyright 2006, American Chemical Society), (k) balls (Mn2O3) (Reprinted with permission.60 Copyright 2016, The Royal Chemical Society), and (l) branches (TiO2-SnO2) (Reprinted with permission.61 Copyright 2013, The Royal Chemical Society). 
Multipods, exhibiting high aspect ratio, are composed of large sphere-like cores with long arms of uniform width and rounded ends which extend out from the cores. Most often the number of arms is in the range from 4 to 8. Their length is measured from the tip to the base, whereas the width is determined in the centre area of the multipod arms.22,53
Cones have height and diameter of the same order of magnitude. It should be noted that they are mainly made of carbon and occur e.g. on the surface of natural graphite.62 In turn, balls are well-ordered spherical nanostructures possessing dendritic exteriors. They can be formed by the aggregation of small particles, which causes their surface to be rough.60
Hierarchical nanostructures are defined as integrated architectures consisting of low-dimensional sub-units, e.g. 0D dots, spheres, 1D wires, rods or tubes, and 2D sheets which are arranged in a well-ordered manner. They mimic structures widely found in nature such as trees with trunks and branches, plant flowers composed of petals, and sea urchins whose surface is spiny.63,64 Synthesis of materials with hierarchical nanostructures gives the opportunity to precisely adjust their physicochemical properties for specific applications. Flower-like structures having size in the range 100–500 nm consist of several layers of inteconnected petals with relatively small thickness and different shapes.17,22,57,65 All petals are dense at the cores and radiate from the centre of flowers to the outside surface. Their shape and dimensions are very difficult to control during synthesis. Flowers are characterized by high surface-to-volume ratio and demonstrate a large number of adsorption sites as well as good charge transfer. This type of architecture can increase the use of nanomaterials in the design of novel catalysts, solar cells, biosensors (detection of conditions such as food infection, diabetes, Parkinson's disease, Alzheimer's disease, etc.), devices, fuel cells and drug delivery agents.17,22,65
Among the 3D nanostructures, branches have been widely studied due to their straightforward modification and applicability to various types of chemical compounds. They are composed of small arms growing radially from the major frame. Both the morphology and length of branches influence the physicochemical properties of nanomaterials. Many synthetic strategies have been described in the literature for branch-like structures, e.g. the formation of branches on a nanowire backbone by a hydrothermal method or chemical vapor deposition (CVD), and preparation of hyper-branched structures via thermal evaporation. Branches that are less densely packed grow depending on pre-deposited seeds. Together with trees, they are examples of fractal structures.61,66 Urchin-like structures are covered with radially oriented and interconnected needles of high density. They are a bit similar to multipods, but urchins have a much larger number of arms attached to the spherical cores.59,67
Despite the huge efforts to control the growth mechanism of three-dimensional nanostructures, some fundamental phenomena are not well understood. Therefore exploration of the impact of size and morphology on their physicochemical properties is still required in order to plan the optimal application of these nanomaterials.17,21,22,25
3. Morphology control of metal oxide nanostructures
3.1. General aspects
MOs are an important class of inorganic materials with diverse composition and morphology. They are widely used in the design of catalysts,68–74 sensors,75–78 solar cells,79–83 adsorbents of many contaminants,84–87 drug delivery agents,88–91 and additives for skincare products.92–95 Significant efforts over the last few years have focused on the synthesis of MO nanostructures with controlled morphology that exhibit new interesting optical, magnetic, thermal, and catalytic properties and thus can enable novel applications. Unique physicochemical properties of MO nanoparticles originate from their small size and high density of the corner or edge surface sites in conjunction with a shape effect.73,74,96 Moreover, the exposed facets of the MO nanocrystals and the high specific surface area can strongly determine their surface chemistry.96–99
From the scientific point of view, in order to fully exploit the potential of MO nanomaterials, it is necessary to understand the relation between their shape, size and the resulting properties. Accordingly, the synthesis of uniform oxide nanoparticles with precise control of morphology is crucial to characterize their specific properties. In addition, shape-controlled MO nanocrystals can be fundamental building blocks for the bottom-up assembly of novel nano- and microstructures.96,100–102 It should be noted that reducing the particle size of MOs to the nanometric scale causes their properties to be governed by high surface area-to-volume ratio and quantum confinement effects. As such, the atoms at the surface of MO nanoparticles dominate over the internal atoms. Changes in thermodynamic stability associated with decreasing size to the nanometre regime can bring about a modification of cell parameters or structural transformations. In some cases, the oxide nanoparticles can be degraded due to interactions with their surrounding environment, high surface free energy, and the large surface curvature.103 Therefore, to exhibit mechanical or structural stability, the surface free energy of nanoparticles must be lowered. As a result, phases having low stability in bulk materials can become very stable in nanostructures. Additionally, it has been revealed that the electronic structure of oxide nanomaterials could be altered from continuous electronic bands to discrete or quantized electronic levels. The size of the nanocrystals has also a significant influence on the electron-transport process.102–104 Similarly, the shape of the MO nanoparticles plays a key role in the determination of their properties. It depends on the crystalline structure of MO nanomaterials because the growth of primary particles is imposed by the symmetry and dimensionality of the structure. The orientation of the crystal facets can be adjusted with the surface atomic arrangement and coordination.98 With reducing particle size, the surface area-to-volume ratio increases and the shape could impart its own effect on the surface structure.96 Many nanostructures of MOs including spheres, cubes, octahedra, polyhedra, wires, rods, tubes, belts, sheets, platelets and complicated hierarchical structures (e.g. flowers, branches) have been reported in the literature. As mentioned in Section 2, they are classified as zero-dimensional (0D), one-dimensional (1D), two-dimensional (2D) and three-dimensional (3D).
Controlled synthesis of MO nanostructures is the main strategy for achieving desired shape and size. So far, many valuable reviews21,22,96,99,101,102,104–106 have described the preparation methods of oxide nanomaterials with tunable morphology, but the topic has not been completely explored. Due to the extensive scope of the synthetic approaches, as well as the diversity of MO nanostructures and composition, a huge challenge is to create a general algorithm that would facilitate the selection of the appropriate method of synthesis and its conditions in order to obtain a specific shape and size of nanoparticles. It is necessary to compile literature data that will allow for a deep analysis of what parameters in the chosen preparation method have the greatest impact on the morphology of MO nanomaterials.
3.2. Synthetic strategies for metal oxide nanostructures with controlled morphology
MO nanomaterials can be fabricated with the use of two general strategies: bottom-up and top-down (Fig. 9).100,101,105 These synthetic strategies differ in the way how a specific nanostructure is created. In the bottom-up approach, MO nanomaterials are obtained from basic building blocks (e.g. atoms or molecules) that react and grow in size or self-assemble into a more complex structure. In contrast, the top-down approach involves breaking down bulk materials (e.g. cutting, grinding or etching) to the nanoscale structures.38 The advantage of the top-down approach is high yield, but it requires expensive, complex and specialized equipment to maintain specific conditions including pressure, temperature or environment. Moreover, it should be noted that the obtained nanostructures often have surface defects and non-uniform shapes which limit their applicability. The control over the size and shape of MO nanoparticles through the top-down strategy is very difficult.101,102,105 Therefore, the bottom-up wet chemical methods are often selected for preparation of MO nanostructures due to their simplicity, reproducibility, low energy consumption, easily available and cheap equipment as well as the theoretical possibility of precisely controlling all dimensions, morphology, and monodispersity of the products.21,22,101,105,106 In the wet chemical syntheses, the nucleation and growth kinetics of the seeds strongly influence the shape of the MO nanostructures. In order to control the size and morphology of the nanocrystals some critical reaction parameters such as the temperature, time, precursor and surfactant concentration, and the molar ratio of precursor to capping ligand should be precisely adjusted in the crystal nucleation-growth stages.
 Fig. 9  Bottom-up and top-down synthetic strategies. 
Understanding the formation mechanisms of nanoparticles is the key to manipulate the morphology. The creation of nanoparticles can be described by the classical Ostwald ripening mechanism, in which the growth of larger particles at the expense of smaller ones is driven by surface energy reduction. This phenomenon is primarily applied to explain the fabrication of thermodynamically stable nanocrystals with nearly spherical shapes.96,99 In the case of the controlled self-assembly of nanoparticle building blocks into more complex anisotropic nanostructures, organic capping reagents usually play a crucial role in reducing the activity of the nanocrystal surface. The organic ligand with high nanoparticle binding affinity can promote or adjust an oriented-assembly, driven by the interaction between surface-adsorbed ligands instead of between the particles themselves. The selective adsorption of the capping molecules (e.g. fatty acids, alkyl amines, amino acids, trioctylphosphine oxide, block copolymers) onto specific crystal facets of the growing nanoparticles can result in controlling the product morphology.21,22,96,99,101,105,106
In recent years, many studies have been dedicated to developing various synthetic approaches that allow the simultaneous control of the size, shape, and monodispersity of MO nanostructures with high potential applications in biomedicine, catalysis, optical sensing, and energy conversion technology. In this section, we focus on the presentation of the most important methods of MO synthesis (e.g. sol–gel, hydro/solvothermal, biogenic, thermolysis, reverse micelle, electrodeposition, two-phase route, flame spray pyrolysis). Their different aspects such as advantages, limitations and product morphology are presented in Table 1.
