Overall, it is obvious that MOFs, MO-derived MOFs and MO/MOF combinations are significant tools to fabricate tailor-made carbon materials for various applications. Certainly, important developments in the field can be expected in the future.
7. Conclusion and perspectives
Structural and morphological control over multiple length scales is one of the most fascinating and important goals of modern solid-state and materials chemistry. Chemists have learned how to control chemical and structural features with atomic precision and thus the properties of a large variety of porous materials. The chemical control over porous molecular structures of MOF materials has reached a level of sophistication that allows for a wide spectrum of applications. Such porous materials can be developed in the form of films, membranes, monoliths, submicron particles or powders that offer many different physicochemical properties that are obtained by combining their porous texture (accessibility, pore volume, specific surface area, surface area-to-volume ratio) with the intrinsic characteristics of the matrix (mechanical properties, adsorption, chemical functionality, etc.). Although these hybrid materials have unprecedented degree of structural and functional tunability, which should lead to breakthroughs in many key technologies, they have been rarely considered in industry so far.
In general, structuring porous materials (i.e. morphology control at different length scales) gives the opportunity to improve the material performance via design of the physical form rather than altering the chemical component. Besides the unique chemical designability of MOF materials at the molecular and nano length scales, we wish to foster pronounced attention for the creation of architectures at the mesoscopic/macroscopic scale by the development of innovating bottom-up and/or top-down strategies in order to increase the physical designability of those materials.
This aspect was taken into consideration by using MO as a precursor for synthesizing MOF structures, as they provide new opportunities for morphology control of MOF/MDC. Thereby, MOs play multiple roles, including architecture-directing agents (templates), cation reservoirs (metal ion source), preferential reaction sites for heterogeneous nucleation, and functional components in the final composite materials. As we have shown in this review the morphology control of MO materials at various length scales is an extremely large and sophisticated area, an impressive plethora of symmetrical and asymmetrical 0D, 1D, 2D, 3D, and hierarchical architectures can be precisely synthesized. In addition, MOs can be easily integrated with various functional substrates and host materials, and pre-installed on specific locations to make desired patterns.
Starting from morphology control of MO itself, the transformation of MO into MOFs and finally MDCs enables unprecedented structures and morphologies. As the predefined structures and morphologies of MOs can be transferred to MOFs and MOF derivatives, the MO-to-MOF conversion strategy will enable the preparation of MOFs/MDCs with a high level of morphological diversities. Of particular advantage in this approach is the ability to fabricate complex morphologies with multiple levels of structural hierarchies which may be otherwise difficult to achieve.
In catalysis and sensing, the surface area is of particular importance for high accessibility of the active centres of catalysts or sensors. Therefore, high reaction rates and sensitivity can be introduced to MDCs during the MO-to-MOF-to-MDC transformation. Moreover, the material morphology has a remarkable impact on molecular transport around the surface, which is a way to regulate diffusion-controlled processes. The pore structure is of considerable interest for tailoring substrate specificity or sensor selectivity, e.g. to allow pore access only for specific molecules.
Morphology controlled MDCs have shown great potential in a number of applications such as supercapacitors, batteries, fuel cells, catalysis, sensing, nanomedicine and adsorption. In energy-related applications surface area is a major feature for MDC electrode materials as it significantly influences the charge and discharge rates – one of the major performance indicators in modern energy storage. MDCs are of particular utility for tailored porosity and pore structures that mediate ion access to electrodes and allow improving exchange kinetics. The morphology of MDCs has a significant influence on the overall efficiency of energy-related devices especially when the volume of the device is relevant.
In nanomedicine, drug loading and designed release show a significant relation to the well-developed surface area and pore structure, allowing the design of release characteristics through structural tailoring by means of functionalization. Furthermore, in the MOF/MDC scaffold, incorporated metal ions can be utilized directly for photothermal therapy and enable application in diagnostic tasks, e.g. imaging and sensing. The MDC systems are a promising approach for theranostics. The classical application of adsorption is mainly related to surface area and surface chemistry of MDCs. This feature can be easily tailored via MO/MOF compositions. With the specificity of adsorption being closely related to surface chemistry and pore structures, respectively, MOF precursors allow the transfer of designed adsorption processes. Regarding the relation of MO/MOF/MDC with the aforementioned applications, a deep understanding of structure–property relationships is missing so far, where future research will have a significant impact. Although considerable progress has been made in this research field over the past decade, there are still many challenges and limitations that need to be resolved in the future.
Higher level of control over morphology, size and composition of MOFs/MDCs is greatly desirable. A considerable part of the literature relies on the use MO nanorod-arrays grown on 2D substrates to form 3D architectures. Future research should focus on the development of sophisticated 3D architectures constructed by various building blocks (MO, MOFs, carbon, and substrates). For instance, bio-inspired complex hierarchical architectures that are pre-encoded with unique functions are of great interest. In addition, controllable design and integration of different kinds of MO/MOF/MDC are essential to achieve collective and synergistic effect from the individual components for target applications. However, in many cases, the parent MOs remain in the final composite materials after the transition to MOFs or MDCs, but barely contribute to performance enhancement. They merely occupy the volume of the composites (devices), and this dead volume is a significant problem in applications where volumetric performance is relevant, e.g. in portable electronics. In this regard, precise material design is desired to merge the advantage of each component into one hybrid material.
In-depth understanding of kinetic and thermodynamic aspects of MO-to-MOF conversion is highly required for the preservation of the morphology of the parent material, with many competing rates influencing the conversion kinetics and hence the transition mechanism (dissolution crystallization vs template-assisted transition). In particular, the balance between the release rate of metal ions from MO and the coordination rate of metal ions with ligands is critical for controlled MOF deposition on MOs. The other reaction parameters (e.g. solvent composition, temperature and pH) as well as the inherent nature of MOs (e.g. chemical composition, morphology, size, surface roughness, and surface charge) also greatly affect the MO-to-MOF conversion. As a result, only a few MOs (e.g. ZnO, CoOx, CuOx) are widely utilized for the fabrication of ‘typical’ MOFs so far, such as ZIFs, MILs and HKUST-1. A combination of theoretical calculations and experimental approaches is highly desired to expand the range of possible MOs. High throughput methods would allow for rapid screening of potential solid precursors and help establish the suitable transformation conditions.
At the moment, the MO-to-MOF conversion strategy is still rather complicated and expensive hampering large scale applications. Development of fast and economical conversion pathways would be highly appreciated and a considerable driver for the development of the field. Direct conversion of low-cost raw materials (e.g. earth-abundant bio-minerals, solids from large-scale industrial production) into value-added MOF products will attract great attention, both in academia and industry.
In addition, the relationships between MOF/MDC morphologies and their properties further need to be established. Although a wide variety of MOFs/MDCs with different compositions and morphologies are reported, there is no systematic investigation of the morphology–property relationships. To study the effect of morphology on MOF/MDC properties, all the other factors, e.g. crystallinity, particle size, porosity, surface area, configuration of carbon and heteroatom, doping level, degree of graphitization, and so on, should remain constant. However, those factors are often mutually interconnected with the morphology itself, so it is challenging to investigate the ‘true morphology effect’ exclusively. Thus, systematic and precise morphology control is highly required to understand how MOF/MDC morphology really affects the properties of the materials in order to optimize the application performance.
As highlighted in this review, morphology control (physical designability of materials) is an effective and versatile way to control the intrinsic physical and chemical characteristics of materials without changing their chemical compositions. MOF and MDC materials already showed extraordinary findings in research in the past decade(s) and preceding research results presented show great promise for further breakthroughs with the refinement of morphological control. Therefore, we believe that this precise control could highly improve the present physicochemical properties, allow the controlled synthesis of highly sophisticated composite systems and add a new dimension to the range of possible applications for MOFs and MDCs. As such, the MO-to-MOF and MOF-to-MDC conversion strategy will play a decisive role, because it enables direct access to complex morphologies that are difficult to achieve otherwise. Although considerable advancements have been achieved in recent years, this research field is still in its infancy, and there are still great opportunities to be explored in the future. We expect that this review will provide a general overview of the previous contribution so that the future direction of morphology control can be foreseen. We believe sophistication of transition mechanisms allowing the controlled transformation of parent structures into desired products over all levels is going to unravel breakthroughs in the development of MOFs/MDCs with defined structures and morphologies that are pre-designed for target applications and device fabrications.

Table Content:

Jongkook Hwang
	Jongkook Hwang received his PhD from Pohang University of Science and Technology (POSTECH) under the supervision of Prof. Jinwoo Lee in 2016. After his first post-doctoral training at the Max Planck Institute of Colloids and Interfaces, he moved to Utrecht University where he worked on development of heterogeneous catalysts for preferential CO oxidation in H2. Recently, he was appointed as an assistant professor in department of chemical engineering at Ajou University, Korea. His research interest focuses on the synthesis of nanoporous materials with well-defined structures and morphologies for energy- and catalytic applications.

Aleksander Ejsmont
	Aleksander Ejsmont received his BS (2016) and MSc (2018) from collaborated universities: Adam Mickiewicz University in Poznań (AMU), Military University of Technology in Warsaw and Lodz University of Technology, followed by a one-year postgraduate research at the University of Central Lancashire. In 2019 he started his PhD studies at AMU under the supervision of Prof. Joanna Goscianska. His research focuses on carbon materials, their synthesis, modification and applicability in fields such as adsorption, drug delivery and environmental sciences.

Ralph Freund
	Ralph Freund is currently a PhD student at the University of Augsburg (Germany) under the supervision of Prof. Dirk Volkmer. He obtained his MSc degree in chemistry from the University of Munich (LMU, Germany) with Stefan Wuttke in 2018, investigating the chemistry of metal oxide to metal–organic framework conversion reactions. His current research focuses on electric-field responsive porous-coordination frameworks constructed from pyrazolate linkers.

Joanna Goscianska
	Joanna Goscianska is a professor at the Faculty of Chemistry of Adam Mickiewicz University in Poznań (AMU). She obtained her Master's degree in 2005 and doctoral degree in 2009 in the field of chemistry. During her PhD studies, she participated in three research internships at Laboratoire Catalyse & Spectrochimie in Caen (France). In 2018 she became a laureate of the habilitation fellowship “L’ORÉAL-UNESCO FOR WOMEN IN SCIENCE”. Her principal research is focused on the synthesis, modification, and characterization of porous materials (e.g. metal oxides, ordered mesoporous silica and carbons) and their application in adsorption processes, catalysis, and drug delivery systems.

Bernhard V. K. J. Schmidt
	Bernhard V. K. J. Schmidt completed his PhD in 2013 with Prof. Barner-Kowollik at the Karlsruhe Institute of Technology and a Post Doc with Prof. Hawker at the University of California, Santa Barbara. Afterwards he joined the department of Prof. Antonietti at the Max Planck Institute of Colloids and Interfaces as a Group Leader. Recently, he was appointed as a Lecturer in Synthetic Polymer Chemistry at the University of Glasgow. His research focuses on block copolymer self-assembly, metal–organic framework/polymer hybrids and carbon nitride/polymer hybrid materials.

Stefan Wuttke
	Stefan Wuttke created the research group “Wuttkegroup for Science”, initially hosted at the Institute of Physical Chemistry at the University of Munich (LMU, Germany). Currently, he is an Ikerbasque Professor at the Basque Center for Materials, Applications and Nanostructures (BCMaterials, Spain). His research is focused on developing methodologies to write and read chemical information onto and from the backbone of hybrid framework materials. In addition, his research interests also include the acquisition of a fundamental understanding of the chemical and physical processes involved in their synthesis and functionalization.

• The possibility of obtaining homogeneous, high-quality MO nanostructures	• Low chemical stability of the product

Hydro/solvothermal	• Straightforward synthesis procedure	• Large scale synthesis is intricate due to relatively high temperature, high pressure and prolonged reaction time	(0D) dot, polyhedron, ellipsoid	TiO2, V2O5, CeO2, Mn3O4, MnO, Co3O4, Fe3O4, NiO, Cu2O, ZnO

• High solubility of the metallic ion precursors in the solvent-contained reactor facilitates the synthesis of highly crystalline oxide nanoparticles	• Impossibility of monitoring the reaction process (because it is performed in autoclaves)	(1D) tube, tube-array, rod, rod-array, rod-assembly, belt, wire, wire-array, wire-porous

• The possibility of obtaining monodisperse oxide nanoparticles that are highly selective and reproducible in terms of purity, crystallinity and morphologies	• The mechanisms controlling the reactions are not well understood

• Size and dispersity can be tailored by changing the type of solvent, surfactant or co-surfactant, addition of electrolyte, water/surfactant molar ratio, concentration of reagents

Two-phase route	• Straightforward synthesis procedure	• The low reactive precursors often decrease the reaction rate	(0D) dot, cube, elongated cube, sphere, peanut, teardrop	TiO2, ZrO2, CeO2, Mn3O4, Sm2O3, La2O3, Gd2O3, NiO, Co3O4

• Slow rate of the nucleation and growth stages which occur at the interfaces	
	
	

• Easy control of size and shape of oxide nanoparticles	
	
	

• The shapes of nanocrystals can be tuned by changing the metal precursor concentration, the nature of the capping agents, the reaction temperature and time

Co-precipitation	• Straightforward synthesis procedure	• Not applicable to uncharged species	(0D) sphere	MnO2, Fe3O4, SnO2, ZnO, MgO	
170–178

• Cost-effective	• Trace impurities may also get precipitated with the product	(1D) rod, needle-like, spindle

• The morphology of particles can be strictly controlled by adjustment of the precursor concentration, the residence time and temperature	
	
	



Electrodeposition	• Cost-effective	• Unsuitable for large-scale production	(0D) sphere	ZnO, TiO2, WO3, V2O5, Fe3O4, MnO2, Cu2O	
48, 49 and 184–192

• Low-temperature operation limited by the boiling point of the electrolyte	
	(3D) floral petal, flower	

• Cheap and easily available equipment	
	
	

• The possibility of kinetic and thermodynamic control by adjusting the current passed through the cell and changing the cell potential, respectively	
	
	

• Flexibility in the control of the structure and morphology of MOs	
	
	

• Formation of the nanostructures occurs directly in the final substrate, which prevents further agglomeration steps of the nanoparticles	
	
	

• Production of high purity materials	
	
	



Biogenic	• Cost-effective	• The exact mechanism for synthesis of MO nanoparticles need to be elucidated	(0D) sphere, cuboid	ZnO, Fe3O4, UO2, MnO, TiO2, CuO, CeO2, NiO

Non-reactive external template	• Straightforward control over the size and morphology of MDC	• Time-consuming multi-step procedures	(0D) core–shell, hollow, single-holed hollow sphere	PS and silica beads, Te nanowires, CNTs, MoO3 nanorods, graphene oxide, metal foam/mesh/foil, carbon cloth, melamine sponge, Preformed MIL-88

• Morphology that is difficult to obtain from MOF self-template is achievable	• Surface functionalizations/modifications are usually necessary	(1D) wires, rods, tubes	

• MDC remains relatively well-dispersed without significant structural/morphological deformation because of the stabilization by external templates	• The reaction parameters should be tuned to attain controllable nucleation and growth on templates	(2D) sheet, sandwich-like	

• External templates either generate internal voids through their removal or contribute as functional components in the final MDC composites	• Inhomogeneous MOF deposition (formation of undesired bulk MOFs in solution)	(3D) arrays (wires, tubes grown on conductive substrates), 3D ordered macroporous structures	


	• Insufficient control of MOF thickness	
	


	• Only a few MOFs can be directly grown on the non-reactive external templates, and their detailed growth mechanism is not fully understood	
	



Reactive external template	• Reactive external template provides additional advantages as well as those of non-reactive external template	• Time-consuming multi-step procedures	(0D) hollow sphere, hollow polyhedral	ZnO, CoO, NiO, Cu(OH)2, cobalt oxalate, cobalt carbonate hydroxide, PAN/metal acetate, layered double-metal hydroxide, and solid precursors grown on carbon cloth, metal foam/mesh/foil, fluorine doped tin oxide glass	
281, 310, 336, 408, 418–427 and 429–438

• Reactive external templates function as architecture directing agent, cation reservoirs, and specific nucleation sites at the same time	• The balance between the release rate of metal ions from the solid matters and the coordination rate of metal ions with ligands should be controlled	(1D) wires, tubes, hierarchical tubes	

• Additional metal ion source and surface functionalization are not needed	• The reaction parameters should be tuned to attain controllable nucleation and growth on templates	(2D) sheets, honeycomb-like flakes, plates	

• The possibility of creating complex morphologies with multiple levels of structural hierarchies which are difficult to obtain from the other MOF-mediated routes	• Inhomogeneous MOF deposition (formation of undesired bulk MOFs in solution)	(3D) flowers, urchin-like, arrays (wires, tubes grown on conductive substrates)	

• Well-established morphology control of MOs can be transferred to MDCs	• Insufficient control of MOF thickness	
	

• Cheap solid precursors from large-scale industrial production or earth-abundant natural minerals are available	• Chemical composition of parent MOs is restricted to a few substances, mostly Zn and Co based ones	
	


	• Considerable lack of understanding of underlying growth kinetics and thermodynamics	
	



Top-down fabrication	• A wide variety of techniques (chemical etching, exfoliation, microfabrication such as electrospinning and 3D printing) are available	• Chemical etching is dependent on the stability of specific MOFs against the etchant solution	(0D) hollow polyhedra, adamantine-like	Chemically etched ZIF-8 and ZIF-67, electrospun ZIF-8, ZIF-67, and MIL-88, exfoliated MOFs, 3D printed Co-MOF	
323, 324 and 441–452

• Pre-synthesized MOFs can be processed into desired morphologies	• Exfoliations are only applicable to a few layer-structured MOFs; construction of high-quality 2D MOFs and their conformal transformation are challenging	(1D) rods, tubes	

• Separation of the MOF preparation and the MOF shaping	• Development of low cost, high yield, and versatile top-down methods is highly desired	(2D) sheet, paper, necklace-like paper, stacks, nanoribbon	

• Any kinds of MOFs with various combinations can be applied in microfabrication	
	(3D) printed mesh architectures	

• The possibility of obtaining MDCs with unique structures and morphologies
